<div id="review_570801330" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570801330" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570801330?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=worldbuilding-sf">worldbuilding-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2019-shelf">2019-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570801330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4449352100270937947">This book very well could be the start of a new epoch. Or at least, I think it should be. <br/><br/>Why? Because it's not just Miéville's grand far-future SF at play here, full of some of the most subtle and freakishly amazing and STRANGE aliens who are very much defined by their language, but because this novel works on several levels perfectly at the same time.<br/><br/>Am I impressed? Hell yes, I'm impressed.<br/><br/>"Before the humans came, we didn't speak so much of certain things. We were grown into Language. After hi</span>
  <span id="freeText4449352100270937947" style="display:none">This book very well could be the start of a new epoch. Or at least, I think it should be. <br /><br />Why? Because it's not just Miéville's grand far-future SF at play here, full of some of the most subtle and freakishly amazing and STRANGE aliens who are very much defined by their language, but because this novel works on several levels perfectly at the same time.<br /><br />Am I impressed? Hell yes, I'm impressed.<br /><br />"Before the humans came, we didn't speak so much of certain things. We were grown into Language. After history we made city and machines and gave them names. We didn't speak so much of certain things. Language spoke us. The words that wanted to be city and machines had us speak them so they could be."<br /><br />Take this literally. These aliens couldn't even conceive of us because their language is the Truth of them. This is the inability to see the ships on the horizon, taken all the way. Lies are impossible, too. Metaphors, doubly so. So when a horrible mistake happens with the new dual/one embassador that manages to actually use the Language to tell a lie, the lie becomes the ultimate drug to the aliens.<br /><br />Enter the collapse of an entire ultra-advanced alien species, with us as the ultimate satanic villains.<br /><br />If you think this is cool as shit, just read the book. It becomes a lot more. And worse happens.<br /><br />The novel works on all levels. Just imagine Cherryh ramped up to Miéville craziness, wickedly subtle and strange peoples and aliens, and let the good times roll in heartbreak, horror, and the terror of having to live with all of your damn stupid mistakes. <br /><br />Yeah, I'm talking about you, Humanity. Jerk.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4449352100270937947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_552591526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/552591526" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/552591526?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330533072" href="/book/show/11955654-embassytown">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=mieville">mieville</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2013">read-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer552591526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17258175732114273580"><b>Proem: In Which an Ambassador Iangrayetiates Himself With His Host With Impunity</b><br/><br/>Is a simile<br/>Like a metaphor?<br/>I cannot espouse<br/>This figure of speech.<br/><i>This</i> not unlike <i>that</i>?<br/>One word a signpost?<br/><br/>Can <i>this</i> be <i>that</i>, or<br/>Would subject object?<br/>How could I <i>be</i> you?<br/>Worse still, you <i>be</i> me?<br/>Well, I know my place,<br/>I'm not one to boast.<br/><br/>I am, like, content <br/>To be just a guest,<br/>Sometimes arriving <br/>First and leaving last.<br/>Not competitive,<br/>Neither least nor most.<br/><br/>A figure of speech,<br/>An Ambassador,<br/>If you please, beyond <br/>Compare and c</span>
  <span id="freeText17258175732114273580" style="display:none"><b>Proem: In Which an Ambassador Iangrayetiates Himself With His Host With Impunity</b><br><br>Is a simile<br>Like a metaphor?<br>I cannot espouse<br>This figure of speech.<br><i>This</i> not unlike <i>that</i>?<br>One word a signpost?<br><br>Can <i>this</i> be <i>that</i>, or<br>Would subject object?<br>How could I <i>be</i> you?<br>Worse still, you <i>be</i> me?<br>Well, I know my place,<br>I'm not one to boast.<br><br>I am, like, content <br>To be just a guest,<br>Sometimes arriving <br>First and leaving last.<br>Not competitive,<br>Neither least nor most.<br><br>A figure of speech,<br>An Ambassador,<br>If you please, beyond <br>Compare and contrast,<br>Bearing messages<br>For you dear, mein Host.<br><br><br><br><b>A Story about Language</b><br><br>In a way, every work of fiction is about language, at least to the extent that it applies language to the telling of a story.<br><br>However, China Mieville’s "Embassytown" is about the very nature of language and how it both separates and bonds people.<br><br>At its heart is a deep knowledge of linguistics (far greater than my superficial understanding).<br><br>However, Mieville’s special talent is to weave this knowledge into an exciting adventure story based on linguistic concerns.<br><br>It’s a fascinating novel in the way some of us might have been fascinated by Umberto Eco’s "The Name of the Rose" or Don DeLillo’s "The Names" (note the word "name" in both titles) or would have been fascinated if a decent writer had got their hands on the religious and historical themes behind "The Da Vinci Code" (not "name", but "code" this time).<br><br>If Tom Hanks can star in a film of an unfilmable novel like "Cloud Atlas", then surely he could help bankroll a film of this novel?<br><br>Now that I think of it, if that novel was an Atlas, then this one is a Thesaurus.<br><br>It’s about what we can learn about humanity from the differentiation, synonymity and antonymity underlying language.<br><br><b>The Language of Diplomacy</b><br><br>Embassytown is a diplomatic enclave in a City on another planet ruled by the Ariekei or Hosts.<br><br>There are human and other Ambassadors and Diplomatic Staff here and, as is the custom, they have to find ways to communicate with each other, despite language differences.<br><br>In the ordinary course of events, there could be disputes, and resolutions have to be developed, negotiated and agreed. It is the nuts and bolts of diplomacy that we mere mortals can only dream of. <br><br><i>"Ambassadors speak with empathic unity. That’s our job."</i><br><br>[I once dreamed of being a diplomat and took a university course designed to qualify me for entry, but they started taking diplomats hostage around this time and I lost some of my enthusiasm. Still, I socialized within a diplomatic community for several years.]<br><br><b>Language As She is a Spoke in the Wheel</b><br><br>To the extent that a common language (such as English) is not used, diplomacy must operate at the intersection of two or more languages. We have to observe and respect nuances and exercise caution so as not to offend our hosts with inadvertent connotations or discourtesy.<br><br>We are always on tenterhooks or tender hooks.<br><br>You can imagine that when two languages first encountered each other, a lot of work had to be done to identify commonalities.<br><br>Was the grammar similar? What words meant the same thing? What are your words for "dog" or "girl"? What are our words?<br><br><b>It Semed Like a Good Idea at the Time</b><br><br>This is where a knowledge of semiotics might help an understanding of the novel.<br><br>Let's use the word "dog" as an example.<br><br>The word is a "sign" or a "signifier", and it "signifies" what society knows to be a dog. The social understanding of the concept relies on convention. <br><br>But a "dog" could mean a whole lot of different types of dog, which are all within the convention. These "dogs" are all within the scope of the "signified".<br><br>The words are therefore signs or vessels that carry meaning that is influenced by society and convention.<br><br>If I say "dog", however, I might be thinking of my dog Charlie, who is small and white, while you might think of your dog, Wilbur, who is big and black.<br><br>Our language is flexible enough to accommodate this personalisation of the signified.<br><br><a href="http://s1111.beta.photobucket.com/user/inksterpop/media/CharlieinBed_zpsc6eb83db.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380412120i/810316._SX540_.jpg" alt=" photo CharlieinBed_zpsc6eb83db.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><b>Ariekei Thought and Speech</b><br><br>Contrast this with how the Language of the Ariekei Hosts operates.<br><br>The word for them is a funnel or a "referent" to the original thought.<br><br>This thought occurs within the mind of a Host.<br><br>Host-on-Host communication is therefore, presumably, much closer to unadorned or unmediated thoughts communicating through funnels.<br><br>If a Host "said" dog, its thought might actually be small, white Charlie dog, and the funnel or referent would ensure that another Host saw and understood small, white Charlie dog, consistently with the thought.<br><br>The meaning or signification of the word wouldn't be [as] social or conventional. It would be more specific to the "speaker" or "thinker". <br><br>Indeed, it’s arguable that there is only "referral" and no "signification" at all.<br><br>We leapfrog the social and conventional, and go straight from thought to thought.<br><br>Hence, the Hosts' "speech is thought".<br><br><b>What Lies Beneath a Language</b><br><br>This linguistic process lies beneath the Ariekei fascination with similes and, ultimately, with lies.<br><br>The transparency of their thought dictates total sincerity, therefore an inability to lie.<br><br>"This" must mean "this" and "this" only (not "that" or "more than this"). <br><br>A simile requires one thing to mean or imply another.<br><br>A simile therefore requires social convention to imply meaning into the words of a speaker that a listener can infer.<br><br>The Ariekei just do not get and cannot replicate this process<br><br>Similarly, the Hosts can't think of a concept without Language.<br><br>As a result, they can't conceive of falsity.<br><br>To be confronted with a lie is an impossibility that is capable of giving them a brain explosion analogous to an addictive psychedelic "god-drug" experience.<br><br><b> Abstraction, Action and Interaction Behind the Language</b><br><br>The process also raises the issue of what they can imagine:<br><br><i>"What imaginaries any of them could conjure at all must be misty and trapped in their heads."</i><br><br>How can they think without words?<br><br>Are they just taking "snapshots" of the Real?<br><br>Can they entertain abstract thought?<br><br>Are they limited in what they can think and speak?<br><br>Is their world primarily one of action in the real world, not so much abstraction within the world of the mind?<br><br>Does the primacy of individual action limit collective or social interaction?<br><br>Can there only be dispute and coercion without cooperation?<br><br><b>Language Channels Into Sects and Cults</b><br><br>This is pretty much the back story of the novel. <br><br>The front story is intimately concerned with these ideas, and to say more would risk thematic, if not plot, spoilers.<br><br>Suffice it to say that a lot happens at the intersection of the two languages.<br><br>And it involves interaction, misunderstanding, dispute, negotiation and more.<br><br>At a macropolitical level, there is a sense in which language is shown to be an agent or vehicle of control.<br><br>How we think limits our potential and our aspirations.<br><br>The restraint on thought breeds obsession, which is channeled through religious and political sects and cults.<br><br>It is difficult to achieve informed unity and community:<br><br><i>"Those rebels must be a fractured community, without speech, if they were a community at all. Language, for the Ariekei, was truth: without it, what were they? An unsociety of psychopaths."</i><br> <br>Freedom and vibrancy require change, and the novel is a dynamic exploration of the change that can occur at the interface.<br><br><b>Woman as Simile</b><br><br>Just as linguistics and political philosophy inform "Embassytown", consistent with China Mieville’s earlier novels, there is an explicit promotion of and support for the active role of women in social and political life.<br><br>The narrator is a woman, Avice, who describes herself as a "floaker", a more dynamic version of a modern-day "slacker" who embodies "the life-technique of aggregated skill, luck, laziness and chutzpah that we call floaking." <br><br>In the eyes of the Ariekei, she is their principal simile:<br><br><i>"The girl who was hurt in darkness and ate what was given to her."</i><br><br>Yet, despite the technical linguistic interests of her sometime husband Scile, Avice is the true social and emotional vehicle for the communication and rapport-building between the disparate groups and the progress of the narrative.<br><br>She is effectively both communicator and problem-solver, not to mention a pretty adept flirt. <br><br>Notwithstanding her lack of overt ambition, she would make a pretty good diplomat, if not one necessarily obedient to the powers that be.<br><br>Ultimately, what appeals to me so much about China Mieville is his ability to juggle sophisticated intellectual themes, genre demands, convincing worlds, interesting characters and well-paced adventure action.<br><br>While the themes of the novel are within my core literary and cultural interests, I admired his skill at bringing the project together with such aplomb.<br><br>I was always conscious that there was a puppeteer making this entertainment happen, but he has an uncanny knack of doing it in such a way that you don’t notice him or the strings.<br><br>I’d like to call Mieville a "floaker" of some sort, but as Avice’s lover, Bren, says of her at the end of the novel:<br><br><i>"You’ve never floaked in your life."</i><br><br><br> <br><br><b>Genesis (A Very Old Woman's Tale)<br>(Thanks to Spanish Dancer and Weaver)</b><br><br><br>In the beginning was god. There was just it, and it was alone. Well, it had to be because it was everything.<br><br>God was a genius, but there is no point in being a god-like genius, unless there is company who appreciates it.<br><br>So god made woman, to reduce its workload. It intended woman to be the origin of everything else in the universe, which had formerly been god.<br><br>Woman would take what had been god and turn it into something else.<br><br>The presence of woman was required to make a difference.<br><br>So woman differentiated between things.<br><br>Having made things, she decided to invent language and words, so that she could give everything a name and put everything in its place.<br><br>Woman rejoiced once this was all done, but it was not enough. She needed a challenge. She thought about it for a few days and nights, at which point she decided to make man.<br><br>She was feeling reckless. Her intention was to make something almost her equal, but not quite, with whom she could flirt, after which she could birth and care for a child.<br><br>Upon the arrival of man, woman looked at him and could not determine whether her project had been a success.<br><br>Woman decided not to make it too easy for man, so she played hard to get.<br><br>Eventually, man worked out that the way to get woman’s attention was to call her a goddess and worship the very ground she walked on, at which point both woman and man lost interest in god, and it retired hurt.<br><br>While woman was birthing, man also lost interest in woman, and never really worshipped her the way that he had beforehand.<br><br>Having espied his face in a pond, man liked what he had seen, and decided to revive and re-make god in his own image.<br><br>He then made a church with other men and excluded woman from any secret god business.<br><br>By this time, woman had realised that when man said she was like a goddess, it was only a simile and he did not mean that she was the real thing, even though a simile is a kind of metaphor.<br><br>Many years later, woman made a man called China Mieville for her own entertainment.<br><br>China is cute, sensitive, strong, intelligent, talented and has tattoos. He knows what a simile is, but he also knows how to treat a woman as a goddess.<br><br>Man is still trying to work out why woman is reading so much genre fiction.<br><br>China Mieville continues to write, while man ponders his predicament.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379778558i/381095.jpg" width="400" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><br><br><b>SOUNDTRACK</b><br><br><b>Roxy Music - "More Than This"</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p9PAuWV-Vn0">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p9PAuW...</a><br><br><b>Lyrics (Bryan Ferry):</b><br><br><i>I could feel at the time<br>There was no way of knowing<br>Fallen leaves in the night<br>Who can say where they´re blowing<br>As free as the wind<br>Hopefully learning<br>Why the sea on the tide<br>Has no way of turning<br>More than this - there is nothing<br>More than this - tell me one thing<br>More than this - there is nothing<br>It was fun for a while<br>There was no way of knowing<br>Like a dream in the night<br>Who can say where we´re going<br>No care in the world<br>Maybe I´m learning<br>Why the sea on the tide<br>Has no way of turning<br>More than this - there is nothing<br>More than this - tell me one thing<br>More than this - there is nothing.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17258175732114273580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_190760169" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190760169" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1886642-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385169623p2/1886642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/190760169?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/1886642-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Catie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer190760169" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2176152306486126648">I wasn’t planning to review this book, but I just can’t stop thinking about it. And then I realized last night that the Hugo Award winners will be announced today and I suddenly had this pang of fan-superstition, like one of those crazy sport people who feel compelled to wear the same socks for a whole week. <i>Maybe if I review this today, he’ll win. Maybe I can speak my wish into reality.</i> See? I really can’t stop thinking about this book. <br/><br/>This book is very different than almost anything else I’ve</span>
  <span id="freeText2176152306486126648" style="display:none">I wasn’t planning to review this book, but I just can’t stop thinking about it. And then I realized last night that the Hugo Award winners will be announced today and I suddenly had this pang of fan-superstition, like one of those crazy sport people who feel compelled to wear the same socks for a whole week. <i>Maybe if I review this today, he’ll win. Maybe I can speak my wish into reality.</i> See? I really can’t stop thinking about this book. <br><br>This book is very different than almost anything else I’ve read from China Miéville. Where some of his other books seem to have this crazy frenetic energy, this constant flood of new ideas and creative imagery, this one feels more focused and streamlined. And while I’ve enjoyed every minute of the high-speed bizarre rides he’s taken my brain on, I fell hard for this story. Like <i>The City &amp; The City</i>, this one gave me time to sit with his ideas. It gave me time to examine them from different angles, to bring them home to my own experiences and make them mine. <br><br>That’s not to say that this book isn’t highly imaginative and bizarre – I mean, this is China Miéville, after all. I tried to explain the premise of this book to my mom last night and I sounded completely ridiculous (she humored me though, because you know…she’s my mom). Those of you familiar with this author will know that you can trust him not to just come up with some vaguely human-looking people, add a few odd characteristics, and call them aliens. His aliens are genuinely <i>alien.</i> The Ariekei have a genuinely alien point of view, which is described with a vividness that can only come from complete imaginative dedication by the author. They have a genuinely alien appearance, which Miéville only partially describes, because it’s just not important. The most important thing about the Ariekei is their Language. Language for them is not so much a way of communicating, but a way of <i>seeing.</i> They speak things to make them real. Everything they “say” with their strange, harmonizing dual voices is therefore literal. I won’t say much more, because I think the way that Miéville reveals each facet of their culture and Language is completely absorbing and I don’t want to ruin that for anyone.<br><br>Yesterday, my friend Maggie left <a>this gif</a> as a comment on one of my reviews and I thought:<br><br><i>I am like the dog in the kitchen, who wanted to eat a cupcake but couldn’t.<br>I AM the dog. And I get to decide why.</i><br><br>Never before have I really stopped to think about how often we speak our truths with imagery, symbols, and lies. Also, this book makes my thoughts sound completely insane. If you haven’t read it, you're probably thinking I’m a crazy person right about now. Which is why this review is definitely not going up on the blog. You’re welcome, people of goodreads!<br><br>Here’s my best shot at describing what this book is about: It’s about a girl who grows up in a distant, alien place and becomes a part of its landscape, its Language. It’s about a woman eventually earns her way into the <i>out.</i> It’s about a traveler who reluctantly returns to her birthplace and finds something to fight for. Like so many of Miéville’s works, it’s about revolution. The path to new worlds is so often paved with blood and wreckage and the bones of lost traditions. But sometimes all that carnage and violence can become the fertilizer for something strong and new. "...<i>the noise of old ways coming off in scabs. Pangs of something finishing, and of birth."</i> I wish I could speak Language so I could say <a href="http://s1212.photobucket.com/albums/cc454/nematome/?action=view&amp;current=CMArieke-1.png" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380354782i/707405.png" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> and express all of that as one simple sentiment. I wish I could say <a href="http://s1212.photobucket.com/albums/cc454/nematome/?action=view&amp;current=CM2-1.png" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380354782i/707406.png" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> to tell you about the journey of Avice Benner Cho.<br><br>-------------------------<br><br>I know this wasn't absolutely perfect but I find it impossible to give it anything but 5 stars. I think <i>The Scar</i> is still my favorite Miéville but this one's an extremely close second. I may write a longer review someday when my life is less crazy, but for right now I can wholeheartedly recommend this book. If you're a Miéville fan, this is one of his best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2176152306486126648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_355090489" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/355090489" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/355090489?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780345524515" href="/book/show/10838795-embassytown">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=nebula-nominee">nebula-nominee</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2012-read">2012-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=hugo-nominee">hugo-nominee</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer355090489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1818498675600846569">Some books are just made for readers. <em>Embassytown</em>, with its focus on the way language shapes our perceptions and our thoughts, is one such book. As readers we are conoisseurs of language, we inhale it and revel in it and cultivate it and all of its diversity. Language informs us, sways us, entertains us, engages us … it is everything to us.<br/><br/>Science fiction seems, to me, like a perfect vehicle for exploring our dependence upon language. After all, there has been a great deal of speculation about h</span>
  <span id="freeText1818498675600846569" style="display:none">Some books are just made for readers. <em>Embassytown</em>, with its focus on the way language shapes our perceptions and our thoughts, is one such book. As readers we are conoisseurs of language, we inhale it and revel in it and cultivate it and all of its diversity. Language informs us, sways us, entertains us, engages us … it is everything to us.<br><br>Science fiction seems, to me, like a perfect vehicle for exploring our dependence upon language. After all, there has been a great deal of speculation about how we would communicate, if at all, with other intelligent forms of life. Assuming we could recognize that they are intelligent, how do we establish a common frame of reference? It’s not like learning a new human language, where we have common memes and ideas, not to mention a shared neurology and physiology that makes our languages quite similar.<br><br>(I’m trying to come up with other examples of fascinating twists on language but drawing a blank on this hot summer day. There’s the <a href="http://en.memory-alpha.org/wiki/Darmok_%28episode%29" rel="nofollow noopener">episode of TNG</a> featuring a language based entirely on metaphors. Feel free to add more examples in the comments!)<br><br>OK, I dug out the fan and am ready to continue.<br><br>Basically, <em>Embassytown</em> is about the quixotic relationship between humans and the Arieke, or Hosts. Unlike any other species thus far discovered, the Arieke vocalize out of two holes instead of one. Their simultaneous vocalization forms singular words and phrases—and for the Arieke, Language is literal in the sense that words don’t actually signify anything other than themselves. As a consequence, Arieke cannot lie, because they can only speak of what is. They don’t have the words to do otherwise. To use figurative, comparative language, they need living examples—similes. These are people who do or have done something that can serve as a comparison for the state the Arieke wants to refer to, but that person has to be present when the Arieke wants to make such a statement.<br><br>Avice Benner Cho grows up somewhat feral on the streets of Embassytown. When she reaches adulthood, she becomes an immerser—some kind of spaceship pilot or navigator—and leaves the planet behind, returning only at the behest of a man she meets and marries, because he is obsessed with the Hosts and Language. Through Avice we see the complicated relationships between the people of Embassytown, the Staff at the embassy, and the clone Ambassadors who replicate Language as best as humans can. Avice is a simile; she is an outsider; and she is also a native.<br><br>I struggled a lot with <em>Embassytown</em>. Newcomers to Miéville might chalk that up to his writing and to the difficulty of understanding what he means as he discusses Language and the ways the Hosts differ from us. I know better, though—it’s not Miéville’s ideas at all that are the problem; they are grand and wonderful and truly thought-provoking at times. No, it’s his characters. At least for me, the problem has and always will be his characters. I don’t know if it existed in <em>Perdido Street Station</em> and I’ve only gradually clued into it, but I noticed it with Bellis in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81202570" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <em>The Scar</em>
</a>, and it was far too obvious in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111093395" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <em>Iron Council</em>
</a>.<br><br>Avice just spends most of the book not doing anything.<br><br>She has an interesting, albeit confusing incident at the beginning of the book as a child. Then she skips planet for a few years, growing older, meeting people, returning to Embassytown with Scile in tow. But she’s always on the edge of the story, watching things happen, passive. It annoys me, these sorts of protagonists. I want to run up to them on the street, grab them by their shoulders, and say, “You’re letting the story pass you by! Go do something!”<br><br>Eventually, towards the end of the book, Avice takes my advice. She finally clears her head, realizes there is a crisis going on, and develops a plan. It’s a damn fine plan, if I say so myself, and what’s even better is that it works … mostly. Watching Avice step up, take charge, and take the lead was the best part of this book, and it really recharged my flagging interest. I just wish it had happened <em>a lot</em> sooner.<br><br>The crisis, by the way, is also quite clever. Somewhat reminiscient of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51682371" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <em>Snow Crash</em>
</a>, it involves rendering the Language into a kind of drug that infects the Arieke (not to mention their genetically-engineered technology). This dramatically changes the status quo on the planet in a way not even the instigators of the plot had predicted, destabilizing diplomatic relations and leading to the brink of war—as well as civil war. Avice’s solution involves radical alterations to the way Arieke use Language. It’s revolutionary but necessary.<br><br>In this respect it’s obvious that the Arieke are in for a big change as a result of Avice’s interference. Yet I never got a clear sense of what they are leaving behind. Miéville describes the Arieke language and the barriers to communication it creates, but he spends precious little time devoted to descriptions of Arieke culture and society. How are they stratified? What is their history like? Do they have spaceflight of their own? The only cultural event we ever see is the Festival of Lies, and that is an artifact of human–Arieke contact, not something indigenous to them. Without delving deeper into the nature of Arieke society, Miéville’s portrayal of them is little more than the background necessary for flogging his linguistic speculations.<br><br><em>Embassytown</em> has all the makings of a good book, but I just didn’t enjoy it as a story. Miéville is a great storyteller—he knows how to break people down and build them up again; and he can make bad things happen like no one’s business. But in this case, there was nothing here into which I really sank my teeth. Avice was not, for the most part of the book, compelling as a protagonist. None of the minor characters held my attention. So I wandered, bereft of an anchor, through a sea of explanation and exposition about the Arieke and Language. It was like reading an interesting but fictional textbook on an alien culture.<br><br>Judging from other reviews, there’s definitely love to be had here if you can rustle up more sympathy for the characters or more interest in what’s happening. I just kind of let it pass me by. I suppose this lack of enthusiasm is what everyone who regularly does not enjoy China Miéville books feels. There were good moments, exciting moments, but for the most part <em>Embassytown</em> read somewhat like the Language it’s about: words without a lot of meaning.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons BY-NC License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380238653i/681550.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="1818498675600846569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_518747185" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/518747185" />
    <a title="Arielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6762949-arielle"><img alt="Arielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452561487p2/6762949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/518747185?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arielle" href="/user/show/6762949-arielle">Arielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6762949-arielle?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer518747185" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7991540108422146225">Embassytown is that rare thing in recent literature: unique. I'm sure there must be other books, other stories that deal with similar ideas, but I have yet to come across anything that comes close to the beautiful strangeness of this book. <br/><br/>There are cons: <i>Embassytown</i> is far from perfect. Like all of Mieville's work that I've read so far, it is hard work (especially at the start) but it does get easier as the story begins to grip you. This is not a comfortable, lazy read. Sometimes I found that t</span>
  <span id="freeText7991540108422146225" style="display:none">Embassytown is that rare thing in recent literature: unique. I'm sure there must be other books, other stories that deal with similar ideas, but I have yet to come across anything that comes close to the beautiful strangeness of this book. <br /><br />There are cons: <i>Embassytown</i> is far from perfect. Like all of Mieville's work that I've read so far, it is hard work (especially at the start) but it does get easier as the story begins to grip you. This is not a comfortable, lazy read. Sometimes I found that that languaging was so alien - or conversely, so familiar - that I was jolted right out of this strange world and found myself contemplating the language choice rather than the story. This is not necessarily a negative; it could, in fact, be part of the author's intended reaction. "Language" is such a deep part of the plot that there is no reason to discount the possibility that the very writing itself was used in such a way to make the reader think about our own human forms of language. <br /><br />Be that as it may, it was distracting. Coupled with this was the tendency to write as though the reader was already knowledgeable of the world and universe in which <i>Embassytown</i> is set. A second re-reading of the book may be in order, because there are so many parts from the beginning that I didn't comprehend and I think that detracted from the latter parts of the story. That being said, I did in fact enjoy the fundamental story so much that I will happily undertake a re-read in the near future.<br /><br />Because oh, the story! The <i>ideas!</i> A dissection, an exploration, a near poetic discovery of what constitutes language and sentient interaction - this is at the heart of <i>Embassytown.</i> There are many other threads woven throughout (addiction, politics, oppression, morality, the essence of humanity) but Language is the core. I will never again be able to look at similies and metaphors in the same light. As with <i>Railsea,</i> the ending hit me so hard it was almost physical. In fact, the ending - if I may go so far as to say - was perfect.<br /><br />I will be very surprised if this never finds its way to motion image - whether in film or television (or even pure art) form. Like the world in which it is set, <i>Embassytown</i> is ruggedly, alienly beautiful; flawed and (mostly) imperfect; unique, and <i>essential.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7991540108422146225" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1526706478" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1526706478" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36819318-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485843897p2/36819318.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1526706478?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/36819318-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1526706478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9943370603903077535">Have you ever been on a first date and suddenly had the sweet realisation that not only are you going to have a great night, but that you're at the beginning of something special, something that could be lasting? <br/><br/>That's how I felt a couple of chapters into Embassytown. <br/><br/>I had no idea what to expect when I began this book, and it blew me away. An embassy district in a vast city on a faraway world. An alien race whose unique language limits their ability to think and entirely prevents them from lyi</span>
  <span id="freeText9943370603903077535" style="display:none">Have you ever been on a first date and suddenly had the sweet realisation that not only are you going to have a great night, but that you're at the beginning of something special, something that could be lasting? <br /><br />That's how I felt a couple of chapters into Embassytown. <br /><br />I had no idea what to expect when I began this book, and it blew me away. An embassy district in a vast city on a faraway world. An alien race whose unique language limits their ability to think and entirely prevents them from lying. A compelling wannabe slacker main character whose day job is guiding starships through a parallel dimension called The Immer. Mieville sews all this into a story that is a riot of color and inventiveness. This is high concept stuff, bristling with ideas, many of which would be worthy of their own novellas, and I ate it up, killing a full winters day in a Japanese hotel room with this book and loving every minute.<br /><br />It's been a while since I've felt the frission of discovery that comes from finding a great new author to read, an author whose back catalogue offers tens of hours of pure reading pleasure (The last author to send a similar shiver up my sci-fi loving spine was Paolo Bacigalupi back in 2014). It's a genuinely exciting sensation, heady with premonitions of lazy, book-filled afternoons and Embassytown has set me off on an urgent quest to read all of Mievilles work. Judging from Embassytown, I'm going to have a shitload of fun doing it.<br /><br />(Oh- and big love to Mieville from me for breaking a three star book drought that was five titles long and starting to kill my reading vibe. This book is exactly what I needed!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="9943370603903077535" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_175134535" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175134535" />
    <a title="Simon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/211278-simon"><img alt="Simon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442471604p2/211278.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175134535?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon" href="/user/show/211278-simon">Simon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/211278-simon?shelf=language">language</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175134535" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4139970023857060918">SPOILERS<br/><br/>There is no subject, not love, religion, sex, music, that generates more quasi-mystical but ultimately senseless gushing than.... language. I liked this book quite a lot, and wanted to like it more; but I was so unable to credit its central conceit, the Hosts' "Language", that I have to judge the book something of a failure. Here are some of my problems with it.<br/><br/>Language (capital L) both is and is not a language. (Fans of the language mysticism in this book might prefer that I wrote the f</span>
  <span id="freeText4139970023857060918" style="display:none">SPOILERS<br /><br />There is no subject, not love, religion, sex, music, that generates more quasi-mystical but ultimately senseless gushing than.... language. I liked this book quite a lot, and wanted to like it more; but I was so unable to credit its central conceit, the Hosts' "Language", that I have to judge the book something of a failure. Here are some of my problems with it.<br /><br />Language (capital L) both is and is not a language. (Fans of the language mysticism in this book might prefer that I wrote the first conjunct of that sentence on top of the second instead of after it.) The Hosts finally make a change, as the narrator Avice puts it, from Language to language. So Language cannot already be itself a language. Yet, Language is spoken, between Hosts and between Hosts and Ambassadors, it enables communication, exchange of technology, so it must be a language.<br /><br />You cannot lie in Language; and its native speakers can only understand it spoken by other natives, or by specially reared Ambassador pairs speaking in tandem. But no means of communication itself makes lying impossible. And how can the Hosts' inability to understand Language spoken by non-Ambassador humans be a feature of Language, rather than their own psychological strangeness?<br /><br />The Hosts make people into similes. I just don't understand what that means. They "say people". I don't understand that either. It's not that somehow the Hosts are incapable of abstraction, and need something concrete, a real person or event, because of inability to abstract. They do abstract and use concepts just fine, apparently. The very similes themselves involve abstractions "the girl who ate..." involved being a girl, and eating, both purely general. In fact, if anything, their limits, Avice intuits, lie in the direction of concretion. They cannot say "that". But their inability is no more a simple inability to refer to particular things than it is an inability to use concepts. They clearly have what philosophers call singular or de re thought. They think and speak about particular things, the girl who ate what was given to her, for example. So they evidently have no problem individuating individuals of various kinds. So what exactly does the ability to demonstrate things, with "that", add? Certainly nothing world-changing; a mere convenience at most."Concepts without intuitions [i.e. singling out of particular things] are empty; intuitions without concepts are blind" said Kant (or was it the other way round?). But the Hosts have both concepts and intuitions. So what exactly is their problem?<br /><br />A lot is made of a distinction between reference and signification. Both terms have been given a plethora of meanings in different philosophical schemes; it was unclear to me what the distinction between them was meant to be here.<br /><br />Well, I could go on; but of course, this wasn't meant to be a text-book in the philosophy of language.<br /><br />The most interesting aspect of the novel, given the failure of its main goal in my opinion, was its treatment of colonialism. The Embassytowners are a colony of Bremen; but they themselves have an initially symbiotic relation with the Hosts that soon turns to one of pure colonial domination when they accidentally make addicts of all the Hosts. I loved the way the Hosts fractured in their responses to this overlordship; some collaborate; some look to free themselves constructively (by going beyond Language); the most frightening turn to self-mutilation to make themselves immune to the power of the overlords. Even here, though, I wasn't quite satisfied; it is the "white man" (actually, woman with Korean surname) who helps them help themselves. Without Avice's efforts, and those of her companions, the Absurd, the army of the self-mutilated, are set to rampage and kill, as restless natives are always ready to. (The woman who teaches them all about language has the initials A.B.C.! One thinks of native children laboring over language primers.)<br /><br />Still, as I said at the beginning, I liked the book quite a lot and will almost certainly read more of the author's work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4139970023857060918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1343115294" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343115294" />
    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1343115294?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330533072" href="/book/show/11955654-embassytown">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=weird">weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=uk">uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=recommended-for-jason">recommended-for-jason</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1343115294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12407048544474142007">China Mieville is not the first writer to tackle the idea of language in a sci-fi setting (I’m thinking of Stephenson’s “Snow Crash” (<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343090562'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>), which drove me insane, and Ted Chiang’s story “Stories of Your Life” (<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1924965094'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>), which was amazing – but I’m sure there are others); but if you know me, you know Mr. Mieville makes me weak in the knees… I read this book when it first came out and recently had an itching to re-read it: my hu</span>
  <span id="freeText12407048544474142007" style="display:none">China Mieville is not the first writer to tackle the idea of language in a sci-fi setting (I’m thinking of Stephenson’s “Snow Crash” (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343090562">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>), which drove me insane, and Ted Chiang’s story “Stories of Your Life” (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1924965094">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>), which was amazing – but I’m sure there are others); but if you know me, you know Mr. Mieville makes me weak in the knees… I read this book when it first came out and recently had an itching to re-read it: my husband is currently working on learning French, which is my first language, and explaining some aspects of the language to him, as well as taking some idioms and expressions apart to make him understand the meaning of some sentences more fully, has really given me a concrete experience of how some words don’t translate – but neither do the ideas they are expressing. Language, after all, is a code for communication, and there is a symbiotic relationship between the way we think and the way we express ourselves, and vice versa. This is an important part of the ideas behind “Embassytown”.<br /><br />This novel is complex, extremely nerdy and intellectual: if you don’t like having your brain going at full speed, put it down immediately. Mieville likes to challenge readers, and this is probably the book where he makes them work the hardest – but I found that process extremely rewarding. I also noticed that despite the complexity of the subject matter, “Embassytown” reads rather quickly! I felt a great love for the Golden Age science-fiction writers in this book (I’m especially thinking of Ursula Le Guin and Frank Hebert here), because while it is set on a distant planet in the far future, it is a very subtle story that uses its (literally) alien setting to put some of our quirks as humans under a microscope.<br /><br />Avice Benner Cho is an immerser, a human trained to help navigate space ship across extraordinarily vast distance, in a far future when the people of Terre have begun exploring and living in other worlds. She was born at the furthest point humans have reached: the outpost of Embassytown, on the planet of the mysterious Ariekei – an alien race that communicates in a complex form of Language which humans cannot emulate. Their only method for communicating with the race they respectfully call the Hosts are Ambassadors, sort-of Siamese twins/clones especially bred for the singular purpose. But the fragile equilibrium of peace between the human colonist and the Ariekei is compromised by the arrival of a new kind of Ambassador, and Avice is the unlikely key to solving this diplomatic and humanitarian crisis.<br /><br />But the plot, while interesting, truly serves as a frame for a conversation about the relationship between the way our minds work and the way language affects it – and vice-versa. The Ariekei are a species that communicates in a polyphonic spoken language and have no concept of lies until the colonists show up on their planet. When that concept is introduced, it begins to affect their perceptions of the world, and changes their behaviour.<br /><br />Mieville’s female protagonists are fascinating to me: never girly, not always likable, but they are very believable and easy to get invested in – even when they do dumb things. Avice is remarkably level-headed and driven. She can be really selfish and dumb at times, just like Bellis in “The Scar” (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/226429615">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>), but the flaws make her so much more relatable and easy to believe in.<br /><br />The world building in “Embassytown” is as complex and captivating as only Mieville does: the alien races and their peculiarities, the space travel technology and methods, the social customs, the perception of time elapsed… He created a world that is truly alien, and that will make the reader feel completely disoriented at first, in the most wonderful way. Once you get your bearings, everything comes together, and while that might take a little bit of time, it is an infinitely rewarding immersion into a universe that brought to mind epic works like “Dune” (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/226433879">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>). There is sometimes a vagueness to the descriptions: the immer is a weird concept to wrap one’s head around, and the Ariekei’s appearance is never truly described, leaving such things to the reader’s imagination, and while this can be frustrating at times, it also adds a complexity, an ungraspability to the world that reinforces the sentiment of otherness.<br /><br />This is a wonderfully strange and mesmerizing book; it will make you twist your brain a bit at times, but trust me, you will enjoy the results of this mental contortion. Superb science fiction!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12407048544474142007" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_740355062" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/740355062" />
    <a title="Penny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10165256-penny"><img alt="Penny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340093730p2/10165256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/740355062?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Penny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Penny" href="/user/show/10165256-penny">Penny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=popular-should-read">popular-should-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=hugo-award">hugo-award</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=bookclub">bookclub</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=scifi-and-fantasy-club-bookshelf">scifi-and-fantasy-club-bookshelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=nebula-award">nebula-award</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=highly-recommended">highly-recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=to-reread">to-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=dark">dark</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer740355062" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15751724747816159165">This may be one of the best books I've read this year. <br/><br/>I wasn't sure if I'd end up in the love-him or hate-him camp since this was my first <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville' title='China Miéville' rel='nofollow noopener'>China Miéville</a>, but it seems I love him! He had been very hyped up by friends and reviews and general opinion and I was nervous that I wouldn't like his work as a result. <br/><br/>The commentary on language and communication was just mind blowing. I could attempt to summarize some of the more interesting points but honestly I think I'd need to read the book a few tim</span>
  <span id="freeText15751724747816159165" style="display:none">This may be one of the best books I've read this year. <br /><br />I wasn't sure if I'd end up in the love-him or hate-him camp since this was my first <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Miéville" rel="nofollow noopener">China Miéville</a>, but it seems I love him! He had been very hyped up by friends and reviews and general opinion and I was nervous that I wouldn't like his work as a result. <br /><br />The commentary on language and communication was just mind blowing. I could attempt to summarize some of the more interesting points but honestly I think I'd need to read the book a few times before I could really do that justice. If you have any interest in the topic I'd highly recommend bumping this up your to-read list.<br /><br />I thought this story was beautifully told and has some remarkable ideas and themes contained within it. I can see how it wouldn't be everyone's cup of tea, but I know which friends I could recommend it to and which I shouldn't. I thought the plot was very engaging and often found myself glancing at the page count and wondering how there could possibly still be more than half a book to go! There were so many subplots that were resolved and then formed part of the larger story arc and all of them came back in the end and the true depth of their meaning was revealed. <br /><br />Brilliant book. Do yourself a favour and read it soon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15751724747816159165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10228894962204502589">Ever seen a Baroque painting? China Miéville is the Caravaggio of literature. He has his own language, his own aesthetic power that might look too grandiose for some, but also refreshingly profound to others. Embassytown is weird. As weird as his other novels, to be frank. We have a living city, with a bunch of aliens who look like insect-horse-coral-fan things, and with two mouths which simultaneously speak 'Language', a sign-system in which the truth of the world and speech itself are indistin</span>
  <span id="freeText10228894962204502589" style="display:none">Ever seen a Baroque painting? China Miéville is the Caravaggio of literature. He has his own language, his own aesthetic power that might look too grandiose for some, but also refreshingly profound to others. Embassytown is weird. As weird as his other novels, to be frank. We have a living city, with a bunch of aliens who look like insect-horse-coral-fan things, and with two mouths which simultaneously speak 'Language', a sign-system in which the truth of the world and speech itself are indistinguishable. Now, I am not claiming I understand everything linguistic here, but the notion that a language, and its complexities, are closely related with power, really fascinates me. Speaking about power, if one enjoys revolutions, political intrigue and subterfuge, then this novel could expand your view.</span>

<div id="review_637513733" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/637513733" />
    <a title="David Sven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8802423-david-sven"><img alt="David Sven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402220807p2/8802423.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/637513733?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sven" href="/user/show/8802423-david-sven">David Sven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780230750760" href="/book/show/9836670-embassytown">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8802423-david-sven?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer637513733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer477371889722885984">This is my first Mieville, and my first foray into his “weird fiction” as he likes to call it. And it is weird - and wonderful at the same time. Embassytown is not just an imagination of new worlds, so much as an imagination of concepts. In this case, specifically, Language.<br/><br/>I had to restart this book three times because I didn’t have a clue what was going on at the start. We’re on another planet, guests of an alien race who can talk to us but we can’t talk to them. Our Hosts have two mouths and </span>
  <span id="freeText477371889722885984" style="display:none">This is my first Mieville, and my first foray into his “weird fiction” as he likes to call it. And it is weird - and wonderful at the same time. Embassytown is not just an imagination of new worlds, so much as an imagination of concepts. In this case, specifically, Language.<br /><br />I had to restart this book three times because I didn’t have a clue what was going on at the start. We’re on another planet, guests of an alien race who can talk to us but we can’t talk to them. Our Hosts have two mouths and “Language” needs both saying different parts of words and sentences simultaneously. Obviously, this is a little difficult when us mere humans only have one mouth.<br /><br /> No problem you say, just use recordings or robots to simulate two voices in harmony. Nope. Our Host’s don’t hear sentences, but the sentience behind the sentence. AI’s don’t cut it, even though they pronounce words perfectly, because they aren’t truly sentient. How about two separate humans preparing their lines and speaking in unison? Still no good. Apparently, our hosts can’t hear us unless Language is coming from a single mind.<br /><br />So how do we communicate with the Ariekne (our Hosts)? How do we do trade? Because they have some pretty cool stuff. They make living machines and accessories. Fliers that are alive. Leather goods grown straight of the tree. Food and necessaries that gets delivered to us through a pipework of living intestines and gets shat out to us through an actual anus on the other end. Oh yeah.<br /><br />Anyway, Mieville builds up a story and world around this central concept of Language (what we call the language of our hosts) gradually rolling out the rules and syntax allowing the reader to explore this new world and concepts. Another aspect of Language is that Hosts can’t lie. They can’t think in anything other than literal truths. How then do you think certain thoughts without the use of say similes? They don’t. They know they need similes to think certain thoughts, but they have to make similes literally true before they can use them. It turns out, us humans are very good for being similes. They make certain of the humans perform specific actions, making them true, so they can refer to them as similes – and so there are humans in Embassytown who are incorporated into Language.<br /><br />The first part of the book where we discover Language, and this new planet, and Embassytown, and how we travel through space, was just fascinating. Even the animals are weird. Like the “trunc.” A small animal that tears itself in two if attacked. The eyes and balls run one way while the teeth and arse stay behind to distract and attack the predator so the eyes and balls can live on to mate another day. Who thinks this stuff up!<br /><br />Coming into the second half of the book a lot of the rules of the world and Language are established to the point that you should be fluent in a manner of speaking that you would otherwise be clueless about. Mieville then proceeds to make Language a simile, or maybe a metaphor, for his own views on politics, religion, political correctness and more. I don’t subscribe to Mieville’s socialist politics or views on most things, but I can at least appreciate how he communicates them as the sub text in the story. <br /><br />The story and plot itself didn’t do a whole lot for me, but they are really only there to showcase his ideas and concepts and imagination centred on Language. I am totally captivated by his style of writing and look forward to Mieville weirding me out some more in his other books.<br /><br /><br /><u>I’m giving this one</u><br />Four and half stars<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="477371889722885984" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2404163539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Paul Sánchez Keighley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley"><img alt="Paul Sánchez Keighley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557221483p2/25180638.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Sánchez Keighley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Sánchez Keighley" href="/user/show/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley">Paul Sánchez Keighley</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330533072" href="/book/show/11955654-embassytown">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=c-united-kingdom">c-united-kingdom</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2404163539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13178166656572824525">Never had international trade looked so much like symbiosis. <br/><br/>Embassytown is a generous serving of yummy brainfood titillating with novum and teeming with grotesque alien life forms. I’m in love.<br/><br/>I mean, you know you’re reading a good book when you’re just over a third of the way in and you’re horrified and appalled and keep thinking this can’t possibly get any worse and it consistently does to the point where it becomes borderline depressing and yet you never lose faith because at all times you f</span>
  <span id="freeText13178166656572824525" style="display:none">Never had international trade looked so much like symbiosis. <br /><br />Embassytown is a generous serving of yummy brainfood titillating with novum and teeming with grotesque alien life forms. I’m in love.<br /><br />I mean, you know you’re reading a good book when you’re just over a third of the way in and you’re horrified and appalled and keep thinking this can’t possibly get any worse and it consistently does to the point where it becomes borderline depressing and yet you never lose faith because at all times you feel that the author knows exactly what he’s doing and that this isn’t an aimless snowball of self-indulgence but a meticulously composed symphony of horror vacui and despair tumbling purposefully towards a very specific premeditated conclusion.<br /><br />If you’re into languages or linguistics, Embassytown offers a lot to chew on. I was constantly fascinated by Miéville’s ability to seamlessly incorporate semiotics and theory of language into the story and not have it come across as experimental. <br /><br />I got a very gratifying <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/95558.Solaris" title="Solaris by Stanisław Lem" rel="nofollow noopener">Solaris</a> vibe from the first third of the book, when we're presented with the near-impossibility of fully understanding, let alone establishing communication, with an alien life form so different from our own.<br /><br />Anyway, I know I’ve read a five-star book when I can't do more than babble enthusiastic gibberish at the end. I’m happy this man exists and I’m looking forward to reading more of his stuff. (By the way, you should check out interviews with China Miéville on YouTube; he’s not only a gifted storyteller with an elephantine imagination, but also a really cool and eloquent guy.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13178166656572824525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
